<div id="review_2163433229" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2163433229" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2163433229?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007306213" href="/book/show/6550370-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2163433229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3339299228861148636">‘The Thing Around Your Neck’ is a collection of 12 stories by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, all of which are uniformly great, although some stronger than others. (Some of which have been previously published separately elsewhere).<br/><br/>As with all short stories and particularly with these, almost by definition – they lack the depth, breadth and sophistication of longer novels – in this case Adichies wonderful ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’, ‘Purple Hibiscus’ and ‘Americanah’.<br/><br/>With the best of novels, the reader is</span>
  <span id="freeText3339299228861148636" style="display:none">‘The Thing Around Your Neck’ is a collection of 12 stories by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, all of which are uniformly great, although some stronger than others. (Some of which have been previously published separately elsewhere).<br /><br />As with all short stories and particularly with these, almost by definition – they lack the depth, breadth and sophistication of longer novels – in this case Adichies wonderful ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’, ‘Purple Hibiscus’ and ‘Americanah’.<br /><br />With the best of novels, the reader is left generally either inspired by, but still wanting more; or entirely satiated. With short stories, more often than not there is a frustration and exasperation with their (almost by definition and out of necessity) lack of depth, characterisation and narrative development – often leaving the reader ultimately unsatisfied and disappointed.<br /><br />Clearly the literary skills required for writing an engaging, captivating and fulfilling short story are much different to those for writing a novel. The scope for depth and breadth, the bigger picture, in a short story must be either alluded to, hinted at, or fulfilled in an entirely different way.<br /><br />What Adichie does here very successfully, is to manage to create a series of wonderfully satisfying short stories – each within a fully realised world of their own. The stories not only feel effortless and have a real feeling of believability and authenticity, but Adichie has successfully managed to create stories that although short, manage to be compelling, engaging and thought-provoking – as well as being satisfying; leaving the reader wanting more in every case…what happened next? But somehow manages to do this in a way that is fulfilling to the reader.<br /><br />The only other short stories that I have read that manage to be so complete and so satisfying (although in an entirely different way) are many of those by Oscar Wilde. Whilst Adichie may not be quite in the same league as Wilde – it is amazing how effortlessly she creates in each case, a fully realised world, populated by such convincing characters and placed in such effective narratives – and all in so few words. <br /><br />Recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3339299228861148636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3456032975" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3456032975" />
    <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads"><img alt="Abbie | ab_reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545340790p2/53055819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3456032975?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie | ab_reads" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads">Abbie | ab_reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/34397251-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3456032975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1746207521504195441">Well, I finished my last Adichie (except for Dear Ijeweale but that's so small 😭) and now I'm upset. As usual, it was golden and now I need any hint of news around an upcoming novel - or I'd take another short story collection!<br/>.<br/>The Thing Around Your Neck is made up of 12 short stories, musing on life in Nigeria and life in America as an immigrant, or both in the same story. I think out of the 12 there was only one that didn't make much of an impression (Ghosts), all of the others completely swep</span>
  <span id="freeText1746207521504195441" style="display:none">Well, I finished my last Adichie (except for Dear Ijeweale but that's so small 😭) and now I'm upset. As usual, it was golden and now I need any hint of news around an upcoming novel - or I'd take another short story collection!<br />.<br />The Thing Around Your Neck is made up of 12 short stories, musing on life in Nigeria and life in America as an immigrant, or both in the same story. I think out of the 12 there was only one that didn't make much of an impression (Ghosts), all of the others completely swept me up. These aren't short short stories. Sometimes I read shorts that are like 4 pages long, but most of these are around the 20 page mark. Within those 20 pages, Adichie unspools lives that feel so full and real that it could have been a 200 page novel. In fact, I would read novel versions of every one of these stories without hesitation.<br />.<br />Some favourites: Jumping Monkey Hill, a woman navigates a writers' retreat with a troublesome host; On Monday of Last Week, a woman working as a nanny becomes fixated on the boy's mysterious artist mother who rarely emerges from the basement; The Arrangers of Marriage, a new wife struggles to adjust to life in America with her less-than-ideal new husband; and The Shivering, a woman in America grapples with news of a plane crash in Nigeria with the help of a Nigerian man in her apartment building who is harbouring a few secrets.<br />.<br />At the end of each story, I felt a combination of sad that it was over but also satisfied. Short stories can often leave you wanting, but a good one will make you want more of the characters while simultaneously appreciating the perfectly formed, slice-of-life story you just read. It's just greed that makes me want more, ha!<br />.<br />Each one reads like a tiny Adichie novel, addressing themes such as colonialism, marriage, identity, immigration, and politics. I didn't save it for last for any particular reason, and I actually think it might be a good introduction to Adichie if you haven't yet read her as it gives a great feel for her style!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1746207521504195441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_760750718" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/760750718" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/760750718?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307455918" href="/book/show/7053591-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fav-authors">fav-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=short-shorts">short-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=africa">africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer760750718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12466036782700489168">I'm so thrilled that before he left this earth, Chinua Achebe blessed West Africa with a younger version of his literary self. Of her first three pieces:( <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus' title='Purple Hibiscus by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Purple Hibiscus</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18749.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun' title='Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Half of a Yellow Sun</a> included) this Adichie collection seems to really highlight Achebe's influence and this is a thrilling thing to see.<br/><br/>Compelling and witty characters, revelatory stories, and just the right amount of sensory elements to help me visualize--just how I like my short stories. Then again, Chimamanda Adichie is</span>
  <span id="freeText12466036782700489168" style="display:none">I'm so thrilled that before he left this earth, Chinua Achebe blessed West Africa with a younger version of his literary self. Of her first three pieces:( <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus" title="Purple Hibiscus by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Purple Hibiscus</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18749.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun" title="Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Half of a Yellow Sun</a> included) this Adichie collection seems to really highlight Achebe's influence and this is a thrilling thing to see.<br /><br />Compelling and witty characters, revelatory stories, and just the right amount of sensory elements to help me visualize--just how I like my short stories. Then again, Chimamanda Adichie is one of my favorite writers and I always end her stories quickly. Fulfilled, yet unhappy--you know, the feeling you get when you've just exited the movie theater and the outside noise and light makes you want to go back and buy another ticket? <br /><br />The stories here are about the Nigerian-American experience, about the Nigerian prison system, about arranged marriages and parenting and newlyweds, about infidelity and empowerment, about individuals and their battles with life's choices. <br /><br />So many thematic undertones and yet because of the subtle storytelling, you don't sense an agenda. <br /><br />My favorites were:<br /><br /><br /><i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i> for its poignancy when relating the immigrant experience even while using the second-person narrative: "At night, something would wrap itself around your neck, something that very nearly choked you before you fell asleep."<br /><br /><i>The American Embassy</i> because oh is this the truth.<br /><br /><i>Ghosts</i>, for everything it says and yet doesn't say about war.<br /><br /><i>On Monday of Last Week</i> because the ending was my favorite part to this story.<br /><br /><i>Jumping Money Hill</i> because it captures the writing workshop so well, even adding the type of incomprehensible light humor you grow to expect and love from Nigerian dramas:<br /><br /><blockquote>She could not tell his age from his face; it was pleasant but unformed, as though God, having created him, had slapped him flat against a wall and smeared his features all over his face.</blockquote><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12466036782700489168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6569745-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54790180-deborah-obida?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1903463276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4374407348449506694">Buddy read with <b>Snezana</b><br/><br/>This book is a collection of short pages, my ebook copy has just 193 pages and it has 12 stories. The stories are extremely short but they are captivating.<br/><br/>Chimamanda is such a great author, she captured the readers with her unique writing style. The stories are all written in different perspectives, two was written in second person, I'm not a second person POV fan but I loved this. The rest of the books were written in third person. The story is easy to understand and it i</span>
  <span id="freeText4374407348449506694" style="display:none">Buddy read with <b>Snezana</b><br /><br />This book is a collection of short pages, my ebook copy has just 193 pages and it has 12 stories. The stories are extremely short but they are captivating.<br /><br />Chimamanda is such a great author, she captured the readers with her unique writing style. The stories are all written in different perspectives, two was written in second person, I'm not a second person POV fan but I loved this. The rest of the books were written in third person. The story is easy to understand and it isn't confusing but comprehensible.<br /><br />The stories all have something in common, all the characters are Nigerian. The focus of most of the stories is immigration, most of the characters believe that if they go to America things will turn around for good, but that isn't true, you still have to work hard, imagine their surprise when they not only have to struggle with finding work they have to struggle with racism they haven't experienced before. The time zone for most of the stories is after the Nigerian civil war so things were hard and most people wanted to escape. <br /><br />Only one of the stories was different and that's the last story <i>The Headstrong Historian</i>. The story is about three generations. The time period was the earliest years of colonization to the time of independence. This story is also my favourite of all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4374407348449506694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10523634680258324306">In most short story collection there are are always some stories that are better than others. Sometimes gap isn't that big (Liu's Paper menagerie and other stories, anything by Bradbury) but there are obvious favorites and weak links and there are those that involve full spectrum from bad to brilliant (any short story collection from Neil Gaiman). This is first time I read collection that I would rate every short story same. Everything is 4 stars range with no clear favorite and no clear weakest</span>
  <span id="freeText10523634680258324306" style="display:none">In most short story collection there are are always some stories that are better than others. Sometimes gap isn't that big (Liu's Paper menagerie and other stories, anything by Bradbury) but there are obvious favorites and weak links and there are those that involve full spectrum from bad to brilliant (any short story collection from Neil Gaiman). This is first time I read collection that I would rate every short story same. Everything is 4 stars range with no clear favorite and no clear weakest story.<br /><br />This is great book to get book for those who want to give Adichie's fiction a try since it showcases here writing well in small bites.</span>

<div id="review_1391020056" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1391020056" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1391020056?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307455918" href="/book/show/7053591-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015">r-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-reality">person-of-reality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1391020056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18291750098396883163">4.5/5<blockquote>
  <i>The first thing that came to Ujunwa's mind was to ask if Isabel ever needed royal blood to explain the good looks of friends back in London.</i>
</blockquote>Look, I'm fully committed to rooting for Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie until the Nobel Prize for Lit committee gets their collective head out of their collective ass and gives it to her (spare me the political yibble yabble. My knowing what's up hasn't killed my excitement yet, so leave me this and go ruin Santa Clause or US democracy or something of tha</span>
  <span id="freeText18291750098396883163" style="display:none">4.5/5<blockquote>
  <i>The first thing that came to Ujunwa's mind was to ask if Isabel ever needed royal blood to explain the good looks of friends back in London.</i>
</blockquote>Look, I'm fully committed to rooting for Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie until the Nobel Prize for Lit committee gets their collective head out of their collective ass and gives it to her (spare me the political yibble yabble. My knowing what's up hasn't killed my excitement yet, so leave me this and go ruin Santa Clause or US democracy or something of that level of fantasy for someone else), so if you're looking for an introduction to an introduction to this writer, look elsewhere. The half star up there is in context with <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i> and <i>Americanah</i>, not the entirety of what I've read thus far, simply because the latter would be extremely unfair to the rest of said works. Only my absolute faves and a few superb fives à la <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52385.Almanac_of_the_Dead" title="Almanac of the Dead by Leslie Marmon Silko" rel="nofollow noopener">Almanac of the Dead</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1447640.The_Guest" title="The Guest by Hwang Sok-yong" rel="nofollow noopener">The Guest</a> would survive. Maybe.<blockquote>
  <i>We are the educated ones, taught to keep tightly rigid our boundaries of what is considered real.</i>
</blockquote>This is the work whose inclusion in the reading itinerary of a particular Postcolonial Short Story class convinced me to take said class. Was it worth it? Looking back, I'd say it did a good job of kicking my ass into high gear in both the Postcolonial, a previously dabbling breed of lack of commitment, and the short story, whose unnerving brevity now has a counterbalancing of a multifarious and sometimes delightful history. In terms of the by rote stuff, I learned the PoCo is more often than not PoMo, the PoMo in a short story cycle SSC is a marvel to behold, and genre stories written in unfamiliar environments are actually quite nice when the author isn't a fetishist with a dictionary looking for that next cash cow. Sure, the nonfetishist author may also be looking for that next cash cow, but the thing about capitalism is you can't expect a cookie everytime you mock the aspects that you personally don't adhere to body and soul. Postcolonial works written by those equipped with an inheritance of the economic type seems a bit of an oxymoron, yes? Bit self-defeating? I work only with the margin that's centered enough to reach my gaze, but if every transitory beckoning every so often reaches the quality of Adichie, I can work with that.<blockquote>
  <i>Grace would ponder this story for a long time, with great sadness, and it would cause her to make a clear link between education and dignity, between the hard, obvious things that are printed in books and the soft, subtle things that lodge themselves into the soul.</i>
</blockquote>I feel the need to include the standard short story run down for the first time for whatever reason, so here goes, complete with quotes around the ones the class chose to peruse:<br /><br />"Cell One" - 3/5<br />Imitation - 3.5/5<br />A Private Experience - 4.5/5<br />Ghosts - 4/5<br />"On Monday of Last Week" - 4/5<br />"Jumping Monkey Hill" - 4.5/5<br />"The Thing Around Your Neck" - 4/5<br />"The American Embassy" - 4.5/5<br />The Shivering - 3.5/5<br />The Arrangers of Marriage - 4/5<br />Tomorrow is Too Far - 4.5/5<br />"The Headstrong Historian" - 5/5<br /><br />My average of the class assigned is a smidge higher than the ones I read on my own, but the class also has the greatest range of scores and has both the best and the worst according to my personal feeling. Anyway, "The Headstrong Historian" is phenomenal, so I will look more kindly on the good intentions of the academic slicing and dicing this one time. For those new to Adichie, if you liked her Nigerian stories, pick up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18749.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun" title="Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Half of a Yellow Sun</a>, especially if "A Private Experience" resonated. If you liked the America, go the somewhat obvious but still great <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15796700.Americanah" title="Americanah by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Americanah</a> way. If you want me to butt out, tackle <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus" title="Purple Hibiscus by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Purple Hibiscus</a>, which I haven't read and as a result you must get back to me about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18291750098396883163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1762381571" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4062319082514811092"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5587960.The_Thing_Around_Your_Neck' title='The Thing Around Your Neck by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Thing Around Your Neck</a> is a collection of 12 short stories, focusing mainly on the lives and experiences of middle class Nigerian women (save for one story, Ghosts that has a male narrator) who are caught up in political or religious violence or coping with unhappy marriages, or faced with unexpected disappointments etc<br/><br/>My favourite story is A Private Experience, in which a Christian medical student seeks shelter with a poor Muslim woman during a religious riot. Their kinsmen (Igbo and Hausa </span>
  <span id="freeText4062319082514811092" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5587960.The_Thing_Around_Your_Neck" title="The Thing Around Your Neck by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">The Thing Around Your Neck</a> is a collection of 12 short stories, focusing mainly on the lives and experiences of middle class Nigerian women (save for one story, Ghosts that has a male narrator) who are caught up in political or religious violence or coping with unhappy marriages, or faced with unexpected disappointments etc<br /><br />My favourite story is A Private Experience, in which a Christian medical student seeks shelter with a poor Muslim woman during a religious riot. Their kinsmen (Igbo and Hausa respectively) are fighting against each other outside. During the time they share shelter, they exchange stories and offer their expertise to help the other. A stream of humanism in a background of riots is an excellent take on the situation.<br /><br />Theme - All the stories are about being suffocated by something, be it tradition or violence or marital discord etc. In some, the protagonist confronts the situation. In some they are left standing at the crossroads.<br /><br />Read a full review as well as my take on the different stories in the collection here-<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.thebooksatchel.com/shorts-thing-around-neck-chimamanda-ngozi-adichie/">http://www.thebooksatchel.com/shorts-...</a></span>

<div id="review_1938893512" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1938893512" />
    <a title="Puck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18562856-puck"><img alt="Puck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498403772p2/18562856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1938893512?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Puck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Puck" href="/user/show/18562856-puck">Puck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007306213" href="/book/show/6550370-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=people-of-colour">people-of-colour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1938893512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8361736640081802865">Wow, what a beautiful collection of short stories! This was my first book by the praised Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie and now I can understand why so many people have fallen in love with her writing. Her style is so mesmerizing and touching that you’ll have no problem getting attached to her characters, no matter how flawed these people might be or how different their lives are from yours. <br/><br/><i>
  <b> “I was happy when I saw your picture,” he said, smacking his lips. “You were light-skinned. I had to think </b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText8361736640081802865" style="display:none">Wow, what a beautiful collection of short stories! This was my first book by the praised Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie and now I can understand why so many people have fallen in love with her writing. Her style is so mesmerizing and touching that you’ll have no problem getting attached to her characters, no matter how flawed these people might be or how different their lives are from yours. <br /><br /><i>
  <b> “I was happy when I saw your picture,” he said, smacking his lips. “You were light-skinned. I had to think about my children’s looks. Light-skinned blacks fare better in America.” </b>
</i><br /><br />In <b>The Thing Around Your Neck</b> you’ll find 12 short stories written by Adichie over the past few years that are often centered around the same topics. Immigration, racism, oppression, the consequences of colonialism in Nigeria (and other African countries), the patriarchy, and sexuality are the most common. Adichie’s characters struggle with these topics in their lives, and while not all of them get a happy ending, there is always a sort of silver lining present. <br /><br /><i>
  <b> “He said you were wrong to call him self-righteous. You said he was wrong to call only the poor Indians in Bombay the real Indians. Did it mean he wasn’t a real American, since he was not like the poor fat people you and he had seen in Hartford?” </b>
</i><br /><br />Among my favorites are “Imitation”, “A Private Experience”, “On Monday of Last Week”, “Jumping Monkey Hill”, and “The American Embassy.” Especially “The Thing Around Your Neck” and “The Headstrong Historian” felt very personal, like they were based on Adichie’s own life experience. They tell the story of how white supremacy affected the lives of Africans – whether they are an native African or living in America – and how hard it is to find pride in yourself and your culture while people constantly try to tear it down.<br /><br />So if you, like me, want to get introduced to Adichie’s writing, or if you want to read a captivating short-stories collection that tackles sensitive topics in a beautiful way, than you should definitely pick this book up. I now can’t wait to read more from Adichie in the future. <br /><br /><b>
  <i> “Are you writing about your father?" the Kenyan asked and Ujunwa answered with an empathic NO because she had never believed in fiction as therapy. The Tanzanian told her that all fiction was therapy, some sort of therapy, no matter what anybody said.” </i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="8361736640081802865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1693068748" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1693068748" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1693068748?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=women-of-colour-2016">women-of-colour-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1693068748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9082444754005373396">4.5 stars rounded up<br/>An excellent set of short stories which concentrate mostly on the lives and experiences of Nigerian women; ranging over issues such as tragedy, political and religious violence, new relationships (especially marriage), loneliness, sadness, displacement and the many problems of post colonialism. There is plenty of social and political comment, but it is wrapped up in human stories. The stories move between Nigeria and the US; the homeland and what is seen to be the Promised La</span>
  <span id="freeText9082444754005373396" style="display:none">4.5 stars rounded up<br />An excellent set of short stories which concentrate mostly on the lives and experiences of Nigerian women; ranging over issues such as tragedy, political and religious violence, new relationships (especially marriage), loneliness, sadness, displacement and the many problems of post colonialism. There is plenty of social and political comment, but it is wrapped up in human stories. The stories move between Nigeria and the US; the homeland and what is seen to be the Promised Land, but seldom is. Two of the stories are in the second person, which is quite unusual. The plain style of the writing better illustrates the subtlety of motivation. There are insights which are sharp and to the point. In the title story, the narrator meets a young white man:<br />“He told you he had been to Ghana and Uganda and Tanzania, loved the poetry of Okot p’Bitek and the novels of Amos Tutuola and had read a lot about sub-Saharan African countries, their histories, and their complexities. You wanted to feel disdain, to show it as you brought his order, because white people who liked Africa too much and those who liked Africa too little were the same—condescending.”<br />Adichie focuses primarily on the Igbo/Biafran experience; unsurprising as this is her background. In each of the endings is a suggestion of a new beginning. The characters create empathy in the reader because they speak of our own hopes and fears as well. Adichie seems to have a passion for human progress and freedom and it is infectious. I’m not going to analyze the stories; just recommend you read them. I think the last one is the best, but would have benefitted from full length treatment. There are echoes of Achebe’s Things Fall Apart, but with Adichie’s feminist twist. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9082444754005373396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_117581624" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/117581624" />
    <a title="Marieke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1386971-marieke"><img alt="Marieke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306621880p2/1386971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117581624?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marieke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marieke" href="/user/show/1386971-marieke">Marieke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=great-african-reads-book-club">great-african-reads-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=west-africa">west-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=nigeria">nigeria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=africa">africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117581624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12420575662362661590">I'm not typically a short story reader, especially in collections like this. But having read both of Adichie's novels (and loved them), i was curious to see how i would fare with her stories. I decided to read one per day during my lunch break, and after two days i was looking forward each day to the next story. Normally when reading a novel i look forward to finding out what happens next. My experience in the past with short stories is that i have struggled to read back-to-back stories by the s</span>
  <span id="freeText12420575662362661590" style="display:none">I'm not typically a short story reader, especially in collections like this. But having read both of Adichie's novels (and loved them), i was curious to see how i would fare with her stories. I decided to read one per day during my lunch break, and after two days i was looking forward each day to the next story. Normally when reading a novel i look forward to finding out what happens next. My experience in the past with short stories is that i have struggled to read back-to-back stories by the same author...I think because the theme changes and i typically can't read two novels by the same author in a row. But interestingly, i found myself looking forward each day to the next story because i wanted to know about what topic or theme Adichie would write about next. She writes in a deceptively simple style that deals with a variety of complicated topics. She has assumed a rather ambassadorial role for African literature in the West (i mean that as a compliment to her). She pulls no punches while swiftly exposing Western prejudices and ignorance for what they are, yet without condescension. At least i didn't sense condescension--aggravation, yes. and some of her descriptions will make you squirm with discomfort when you recognize yourself or your friends or your community in them. I think this type of evocative and provocative writing is important and I hope as Westerners become more familiar with contemporary African literature, we will finally begin to shed our misperceptions of the continent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12420575662362661590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_136286209" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136286209" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136286209?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007306213" href="/book/show/6550370-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-2008">pub-2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136286209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9310372712852047609">I think Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is my more famous Nigerian alter-ego. These stories might not be literary perfect but they completely match my sensibilities. They touch on the same themes that haunt me and my sad attempts at writing - disappointment, self-consciousness, the immigrant experience on the very personal, intimate level. <br/><br/>Each story meant something to me and it would be hard for me to find the one that was my least favourite. I loved those that described the cultural foundation as sha</span>
  <span id="freeText9310372712852047609" style="display:none">I think Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is my more famous Nigerian alter-ego. These stories might not be literary perfect but they completely match my sensibilities. They touch on the same themes that haunt me and my sad attempts at writing - disappointment, self-consciousness, the immigrant experience on the very personal, intimate level. <br /><br />Each story meant something to me and it would be hard for me to find the one that was my least favourite. I loved those that described the cultural foundation as shaky ground shifting under your feet and offering little support.<br /><br />While I liked them all (being Adichie's mental twin and all), I can understand that to some people some of the stories might lack depth and lasting impact. I felt I always knew what she was trying to say even those times when she failed in delivery.<br /><br />Even if it is not going to become your favourite collection of short stories, it is definitely worth reading because it offers a unique insight into Nigerian reality.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9310372712852047609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190518995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2651271408709880084">I can barely begin to explain the catharsis of reading Adichie's prose. In particular, I am captivated by the way her stories respond to the expectations of "ethnic fiction" and "African fiction," as genres full of Third-world starvation and refugees. She deftly handles subjectivities of black African positionality, facets of identity which the market would slam as "inauthentic," or "not African enough." Her stories are delightfully astute, her characters cracking the lenses by which one might e</span>
  <span id="freeText2651271408709880084" style="display:none">I can barely begin to explain the catharsis of reading Adichie's prose. In particular, I am captivated by the way her stories respond to the expectations of "ethnic fiction" and "African fiction," as genres full of Third-world starvation and refugees. She deftly handles subjectivities of black African positionality, facets of identity which the market would slam as "inauthentic," or "not African enough." Her stories are delightfully astute, her characters cracking the lenses by which one might expect to view Nigeria. And they do not rely on resistance to justify their being; they are strong enough to simply exist.<br /><br />Despite feeling wrung out at the end of some stories, I plowed through the collection in a day. I just ordered one of her novels, and am anxious to read more.</span>

          </span>

They're all full of wonderful characters. They're all incredibly full of emotion. Every single one of them felt like it could have been fleshed out into a full length novel. And all of them had such an incredible sense of place and community and the immigrant experience. <br/><br/>Glorious, from start to finish.</span>

<div id="review_3536821874" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007306213" href="/book/show/6550370-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=get-free-bookshop">get-free-bookshop</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-for-blog">reviewed-for-blog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=war-fiction">war-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>,

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3536821874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6309113593407281967">My third by Adichie this year, and an ideal follow-up to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3473103861' rel='nofollow noopener'>Half of a Yellow Sun</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3415221305' rel='nofollow noopener'>Americanah</a> because it reworks or anticipates themes and settings from both novels. For instance, the former was in my mind while reading “Ghosts,” in which a retired mathematics professor meets an old colleague he assumed had died during the Biafran War. “Cell One” and “A Private Experience” picture a Nigeria rife with violence and rioting. The missing are presumed dead and the imprisoned are in danger of being ‘di</span>
  <span id="freeText6309113593407281967" style="display:none">My third by Adichie this year, and an ideal follow-up to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3473103861" rel="nofollow noopener">Half of a Yellow Sun</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3415221305" rel="nofollow noopener">Americanah</a> because it reworks or anticipates themes and settings from both novels. For instance, the former was in my mind while reading “Ghosts,” in which a retired mathematics professor meets an old colleague he assumed had died during the Biafran War. “Cell One” and “A Private Experience” picture a Nigeria rife with violence and rioting. The missing are presumed dead and the imprisoned are in danger of being ‘disappeared’. I was reminded of Americanah while reading “Imitation” and “On Monday of Last Week,” in which Nigerian women in Philadelphia tire of submission to their husbands and make their own life changes.<br /><br />Characters hope to win the visa lottery to the USA, adjust to an arranged marriage, fret over a plane crash back home, or dare to speak out about mistreatment of women. African-American women’s freedom is attractive by comparison. The final story, “The Headstrong Historian,” has the most expansive sense of time: it opens in what feels like an ancient tribal setting – before the next generation attended Anglican mission school. A third-generation character rescues her family’s story, reclaiming her African heritage while taking advantage of Western education. I was especially charmed by two stories in the second person, including the title story, which refers not to a necklace but to a burden of depression. There’s not a dud among the dozen here. Adichie has won me as a loyal fan.<br /><br />Originally published on my blog, <a href="https://bookishbeck.wordpress.com/2020/09/26/short-stories-in-september-part-ii-adichie-shanahan-more/" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookish Beck</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6309113593407281967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2726530363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2726530363" />
    <a title="Monika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38021482-monika"><img alt="Monika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581038684p2/38021482.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2726530363?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monika" href="/user/show/38021482-monika">Monika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007306213" href="/book/show/6550370-the-thing-around-your-neck">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2726530363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16931639578313364508"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>CHIMAMANDA.</a> A name that I heard three years ago. A name that I was trying so desperately to get familiar with. A name, to be honest, I fell in love with instantly. It is also a name that I found on Facebook during a time when she had written what I consider to be her masterpiece. Along with all that, it is also a name that I am glad I didn't try to read three years earlier because had I read her then, I know I would have pretended to like her words because I knew she was 'cool'. <br/><br/>I received <i>The T</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16931639578313364508" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">CHIMAMANDA.</a> A name that I heard three years ago. A name that I was trying so desperately to get familiar with. A name, to be honest, I fell in love with instantly. It is also a name that I found on Facebook during a time when she had written what I consider to be her masterpiece. Along with all that, it is also a name that I am glad I didn't try to read three years earlier because had I read her then, I know I would have pretended to like her words because I knew she was 'cool'. <br /><br />I received <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i> as a birthday present from a 'friend' whose friendship, unfortunately, didn't last long. Chimamanda did, though. I don't remember ever thinking about why I wasn't picking her up even though her book was waiting to be read. Her art of simplified truances were not for me. Now, the story is different. Books have taught me a lot and I have matured enough to bask in the sinful glory of a dreamless haze; sinful because how can someone, in a language that is being used everyday, create something as mesmerizing and as private as this?<br /><br />Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie's collection of short stories, <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i>, is, for me, like a fabled dream. African cultures have always drawn me towards themselves. The sheer monopoly with which writers from Africa or those of African origin carve their stories and tales in words is something with which I will be always biased. It is the history of slavery that had first drawn me to African writings and it is the same history of slavery, of oppression, of British colonialism and of the neo-colonialism that constantly perpetuates strength in me. African history is a history that taught me there is more to 'national' and 'cultural' history than what we generally see. <br /><br />This book of short stories contain twelve short stories by Adichie. There is something about short stories that I both like and dislike - like the gorgeousness of pain, it stays and tingles long after the effect becomes invisible to the peering eyes. Be it the growing up of a boy in Cell One or the opaqueness of future of The American Embassy, the ghosts of Ghost or the contracts of The Arrangers of Marriage, the freedom of The Jumping Hill or the hate of Tomorrow Is Too Far - I want to carry it all. I want to carry Nkem's hopefulness, Chika's searchful eyes, 'you''s nothingness, Udenna's memories, Kamara's cage and Afamefuna's courage. I wish I had it in me to carry all of it but I know that I am too weak to bear all the weight. <br /><br />This short story collection needs to be read by all of us. If it is possible, I'd even say that it needs to be re-read if you could spare a few moments to it. It is devastating, chilling, nauseating and yet it is so beautiful and glorious that it wouldn't part away with your skin. Read it. You must. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16931639578313364508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=african-literature">african-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96516704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18260834819094166172">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie has that rare ability to portray the contradictions of the human condition. Over and over again, she returns to themes of exile, homesickness, and alienation. In the title story, the young narrator gains a prized American visa and goes to her uncle’s home in Maine. “They spoke Igbo and ate garri for lunch and it was like home until your uncle came into the cramped basement where you slept and pulled you forcefully to him…” recalls the barely-adult girl. <br/><br/>Again, in Arrange</span>
  <span id="freeText18260834819094166172" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie has that rare ability to portray the contradictions of the human condition. Over and over again, she returns to themes of exile, homesickness, and alienation. In the title story, the young narrator gains a prized American visa and goes to her uncle’s home in Maine. “They spoke Igbo and ate garri for lunch and it was like home until your uncle came into the cramped basement where you slept and pulled you forcefully to him…” recalls the barely-adult girl. <br /><br />Again, in Arrangers of Marriages, a young bride discovers that all is different in America when her new husband tells her, “You don’t understand how it works in this country. If you want to get anywhere you have to be as mainstream as possible. If not, you will be left by the roadside.” And in Imitation: “She does miss home, though, her friends, the cadence of Igbo and Yoruba and pidgin English spoken around her…”<br /><br />Home is a complex place. The protagonists, mostly young, mostly female, are often a long way from home, both figuratively and literally. Many have fled or want to flee because of violence – a young woman whose four-year-old son was killed before her eyes, for instance, in The American Embassy. But America, in most instances, is not a panacea. A dream of a college education is traded in for the reality of a waitress job. A man whom is chosen by one woman’s family -- “A doctor in America! What could be better?” – is, in reality, a pretentious idiot who insists she speak proper English, change her name, and eat in fast food courts. <br /><br />And so most of Adichie’s characers precariously straddle two worlds. Yet what shines forth is the resilience of the women who take destiny in their own hands and remain unbowed. We meet women who flee from uncomfortable situations with their dignity intact. In Monkey Hill, for example, the protagonist – a writer – is challenged that her character would not give up a lucrative job because she was a “woman with no other choices.” She reflects, “The only thing I didn’t add in the story is that after I left my coworker and walked out of the alhaji’s house, I got into the Jeep and insisted that the driver take me home because I knew it was the last time I would be riding in it.”<br /><br />One of the most affecting stories in this collection, to my mind, is the first story, Cell One, a harrowing tale of the narrator’s brother, who is arrested n a roundup of gang suspects and sent to jail. That story ends, “It would have been so easy for him, my charming brother, to make a sleek drama of his story, but he did not.” The same might be said about Ms. Adichie, who forgoes the drama for solid story-telling.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18260834819094166172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TF/7tGht3i00+TY/fLWfwAAGDwCjxFGKcj+ngRSCl3++/6NbJ8UbwjCn5S6lH4W6b8zcAzNbn/UjcU2WjqXb0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/XMU1KUi05aKRCBvKEOkZY+Gj9z6BU+4Wc5xtQOb9qkP00w76ooWeY4a837x6b4f4Exc32qagccIgJuimby6BA==')})
  }
//]]>

Extraordinary for its exquisite language, its implacable sorrow, its soaring beauty, and for its monumental portrait of the forces that shape families and nations, Homegoing heralds the arrival of a major new voice in contemporary fiction.\n\nTwo half-sisters, Effia and Esi, are born into different villages in eighteenth-century Ghana. Effia is married off to an Englishman and lives in comfort in the palatial rooms of Cape Coast Castle. Unbeknownst to Effia, her sister, Esi, is imprisoned beneath her in the castle\'s dungeons, sold with thousands of others into the Gold Coast\'s booming slave trade, and shipped off to America, where her children and grandchildren will be raised in slavery. One thread of Homegoing follows Effia\'s descendants through centuries of warfare in Ghana, as the Fante and Asante nations wrestle with the slave trade and British colonization. The other thread follows Esi and her children into America. From the plantations of the South to the Civil War and the Great Migration, from the coal mines of Pratt City, Alabama, to the jazz clubs and dope houses of twentieth-century Harlem, right up through the present day, Homegoing makes history visceral, and captures, with singular and stunning immediacy, how the memory of captivity came to be inscribed in the soul of a nation. \n\nGeneration after generation, Yaa Gyasi\'s magisterial first novel sets the fate of the individual against the obliterating movements of time, delivering unforgettable characters whose lives were shaped by historical forces beyond their control. When bloodshed erupts in the city around him, Dantala must decide what kind of Muslim—and what kind of man—he wants to be. Many years later, one sister lives with her black daughter in the same southern town she once tried to escape. The other secretly passes for white, and her white husband knows nothing of her past. Still, even separated by so many miles and just as many lies, the fates of the twins remain intertwined. Looking well beyond issues of race, The Vanishing Half considers the lasting influence of the past as it shapes a person\'s decisions, desires, and expectations, and explores some of the multiple The conflict was infamous for its savage impact on the Biafran people, Chinua Achebe’s people, many of whom were starved to death after the Nigerian government blockaded their borders. By then, Chinua Achebe was already a world-renowned novelist, with a young family to protect. He took the Biafran side in the conflict and served his government as a roving cultural ambassador, from which vantage he absorbed the war’s full horror. Immediately after, Achebe took refuge in an academic post in the United States, and for more than forty years he has maintained a considered silence on the events of those terrible years, addressing them only obliquely through his poetry. Now, decades in the making, comes a towering reckoning with one of modern Africa’s most fateful events, from a writer whose words and courage have left an enduring stamp on world literature.Achebe masterfully relates his experience, both as he lived it and how he has come to understand it. He begins his story with Nigeria’s birth pangs and the story of his own upbringing as a man and as a writer so that we might come to understand the country’s promise, which turned to horror when the hot winds of hatred began to stir. To read There Was a Country is to be powerfully reminded that artists have a particular obligation, especially during a time of war. All writers, Achebe argues, should be committed writers—they should speak for their history, their beliefs, and their people.Marrying history and memoir, poetry and prose, There Was a Country is a distillation of vivid firsthand observation and forty years of research and reflection. Wise, humane, and authoritative, it will stand as definitive and reinforce Achebe’s place as one of the most vital literary and moral voices of our age.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">About Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1474446019p3/11291.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
28,941
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10456956759750451391">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br/><br/>Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won t</span>
  <span id="freeText10456956759750451391" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br /><br />Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won the Orange Prize and was a National Book Critics Circle Award Finalist and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book; and <i>Americanah</i>, which won the National Book Critics Circle Award and was named one of <i>The New York Times</i> Top Ten Best Books of 2013. Ms. Adichie is also the author of the story collection <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i>.<br /><br />Ms. Adichie has been invited to speak around the world. Her 2009 TED Talk, <i>The Danger of A Single Story</i>, is now one of the most-viewed TED Talks of all time. Her 2012 talk <i>We Should All Be Feminists</i> has a started a worldwide conversation about feminism, and was published as a book in 2014.<br /><br />Her most recent book, <i>Dear Ijeawele, or a Feminist Manifesto in Fifteen Suggestions</i>, was published in March 2017.<br /><br />A recipient of a MacArthur Foundation Fellowship, Ms. Adichie divides her time between the United States and Nigeria.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10456956759750451391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
